{"architecture": "PARENT_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_pipeline.HoverMultiHopPipeline\nMETRIC_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_utils.discrete_retrieval_eval\n\n## Overview\nThe HoVer (fact verification) system is a multi-hop document retrieval pipeline designed to identify supporting documents for fact-checking claims. It uses an iterative retrieval approach with three sequential \"hops\" to progressively discover relevant documents across multiple related topics.\n\n## Key Modules\n- **HoverMultiHopPipeline**: Top-level wrapper that initializes the ColBERTv2 retrieval model and executes the program\n- **HoverMultiHop**: Core program implementing the 3-hop retrieval strategy with summarization at each hop\n- **hoverBench**: Dataset loader filtering HoVer dataset examples to 3-hop cases (26K+ training examples from hover-nlp/hover)\n- **hover_utils**: Contains the evaluation metric and document counting utilities\n\n## Data Flow\n1. Initial claim is used to retrieve k=7 documents (hop 1)\n2. Hop 1 docs are summarized using ChainOfThought\n3. Summary generates a refined query for hop 2, retrieving k=7 more documents\n4. Both summaries inform hop 3 query generation for final k=7 documents\n5. All retrieved documents (21 total) are concatenated and returned\n\n## Optimization Metric\n`discrete_retrieval_eval` checks if all gold supporting documents (from supporting_facts) are present in the retrieved set (max 21 docs). Returns binary score: True if gold_titles \u2286 found_titles, False otherwise. Document titles are normalized and matched using the first segment before \" | \" delimiter."}

Iteration 1: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Task Description:

You will be given two input fields: `claim` and `summary_1`. Your task is to generate a `query` (or set of precise queries) that would enable an information retrieval system or fact-checking process to gather authoritative evidence to verify, refute, or clarify the claim based on the information summarized in `summary_1`.

Detailed Instructions:

1. Understand the Claim and Summary:
   - Carefully read the `claim` to identify all individual factual components or assertions it contains.
   - Read `summary_1` to understand which parts of the claim are supported, partially supported, not supported, or unsupported due to insufficient evidence.
   - Notice any clarifications, corrections, or areas where evidence is lacking as noted in `summary_1`.

2. Identify Information Gaps:
   - Pinpoint the exact pieces of information or evidence that would be needed to confirm or refute each part of the claim.
   - If the claim involves multiple components (entities, dates, locations, events, relationships), consider them separately.
   - Acknowledge if the claim makes comparative or causal statements needing comparative data or causality confirmation.

3. Domain and Fact-Specific Considerations:
   - The claims and summaries often include niche or domain-specific entities (e.g., people’s biographical details, roles in films and TV, specific video game engines, botanical nomenclature, sports statistics, institutional data).
   - Take note of proper nouns, relationships, dates, numeric values, and specialized terminology (e.g., "Honorary Doctorate in Psychology", “roman à clef”, "Jupiter EX engine", "Oxford University Dramatic Society").
   - Consider typical authoritative sources depending on the domain (e.g., official university sites, film credits databases like IMDb, music producer biographies, botanical taxonomic databases, official sports tournament archives, airport and geographic data sources, corporate ownership data).

4. Query Construction Strategy:
   - Formulate targeted, efficient queries designed to retrieve precise, authoritative, and citable sources.
   - Where applicable, combine keywords with Boolean operators (AND, OR) to maximize retrieval of relevant documents.
   - Include entity names, key dates or numeric figures, specific events or titles, and qualifiers like “biography,” “official site,” “release date,” “cast list,” “headquarters location,” or “passenger numbers.”
   - Consider domain-specific reliable sites or indicators such as site:wikipedia.org, site:.edu, site:.gov, official company or festival URLs, or reputable news/media sites.
   - Suggest multi-part queries if multiple distinct pieces of evidence are needed.

5. Guidance on Depth:
   - When the existing summary or passages lack sufficient information to determine claim veracity, queries should explicitly try to fill those gaps by requesting concise authoritative data (e.g., exact birth dates, official event participation lists).
   - When the claim is partially supported, queries should verify ambiguous or partially corroborated facts.
   - When claims involve complex relationships (ownership chains, collaborative works, thematic links), queries should be built to confirm the connection thoroughly.

6. Format of Output:
   - Provide a `reasoning` section briefly explaining why you are constructing the queries as you do and how they relate to the claim and summary.
   - Provide a clear `query` or set of queries (numbered or bullet-pointed) and ensure that each query is self-contained, explicit, and targeted for retrieval of the required information.
   - Avoid speculation or rewriting the claim unless specifically requested.

7. Consider Contextual Nuances:
   - Pay attention to nuances such as differences between a "sky goddess" and the "feather of truth" belonging to a different deity, or between film release dates and TV show dates.
   - Queries should reflect these clarifications, ensuring to disambiguate or explore relevant distinctions.
   - Queries should anticipate possible confusions (e.g., similar names, overlapping dates, or similarly named entities) and try to obtain confirmatory evidence to rule out errors.

This approach ensures that your queries help gather precise authoritative evidence for factual verification of claims involving complex, domain-specific knowledge across biography, entertainment, geography, botany, sports, religion, corporate data, and other specialized fields.

Iteration 3: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: You will be given three input fields: `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`. Your task is to produce a `query` field that details search queries or information requests designed to verify or falsify the claim. 

The inputs and outputs relate to fact verification tasks where:

- `claim` is a factual statement possibly containing multiple factual elements.
- `summary_1` and `summary_2` are independent condensed evidence evaluations that categorize parts or all of the claim as "supported," "not supported," "partially supported," "contradicted," or "insufficient evidence," referencing specific passages or information.
  
Your goal in producing the `query` is to identify what additional information, citations, or sources are needed to confirm or refute the claim fully and accurately, given the evidence status in the summaries.

Key considerations and instructions:

1. **Analyze the summaries carefully:** Determine which parts of the claim are:
   - Supported by existing evidence,
   - Contradicted or disproven,
   - Partially supported or 
   - Unsupported due to insufficient or missing evidence.

2. **Decompose the claim into distinct factual components:** For claims with multiple assertions (e.g., multiple people, events, dates, or roles), break down the claim into these components. Identify individually what needs confirmation or refutation.

3. **Identify information gaps:** For components marked unsupported or partially supported, specify exactly what kind of evidence or citations are needed to verify them. This could be:
   - Names, dates, roles, authorship, filmography details,
   - Confirmation of relationships between entities,
   - Historical or biographical facts,
   - Source citations like production credits, official records, biographies, catalogs, or authoritative histories.

4. **Construct targeted, precise search queries or requests:**
   - Use exact names, key phrases, dates, roles, or titles mentioned in the claim.
   - Use quotation marks for exact phrase searches.
   - Include alternate forms or synonyms if applicable.
   - Suggest the types of sources that are typically authoritative for the domain (e.g., IMDb for film credits, U.S. Census for population data, official encyclopedias, or scholarly publications).
   - If relevant, suggest searches combining multiple key entities or attributes to pinpoint verification (e.g., "Actor Name" + "Film Title" + "Role").

5. **If summaries conflict or are unclear, seek clarifications:**
   - Request original passages or citations that clarify disputed facts.
   - Ask for confirmation on names, roles, or key identifiers.

6. **Maintain a neutral, precise, and clear style:**
   - Avoid assumptions or unsupported conclusions.
   - Frame searches or queries as requests for reliable citations or authoritative verification.
   - When parts of the claim are false or contradicted, phrase queries to confirm the correct facts or contradicting data.

7. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge where apparent:**
   - For film claims, reference known authoritative sources like IMDb, official film awards, or biographies.
   - For historical claims, cite recognized encyclopedias, government records, or historical place-name dictionaries.
   - For population statistics, specify census data or official government population estimates.
   - For music or art claims, suggest searching artist biographies, collaboration histories, and discographies.

8. **Handle multi-part or compound claims by providing multiple search queries or numbered query points to address each aspect distinctly.**

9. **When a claim compares two or more entities (people, dates, places), ensure your queries seek comparable evidence to establish relative facts (e.g., birth dates, population sizes).**

10. **Address dates and chronology critically, requesting precise birth dates, release dates, or event timelines as needed to verify sequences or precedence claims.**

In summary, your `query` output should be a comprehensive, methodical set of targeted searches or citation requests structured to fill evidential gaps, confirm disputed facts, or obtain authoritative support needed to resolve the truthfulness of the claim considering the information from `summary_1` and `summary_2`.

Entity chains are sequences of related entities mentioned in the claim (e.g., person -> organization -> location).

This listwise ranking approach allows you to make direct comparisons between documents to identify the most relevant ones.
Prioritize documents that:

- Contain factual evidence rather than tangential information

Iteration 5: Proposed new text for program.doc_scorer.predict: You will be given, for each example, the following inputs:

1. A **claim**: a factual statement or question involving one or more named entities, concepts, or assertions that need to be verified or assessed for relevance.

2. A set of **entity chains**: semantic paths or knowledge graphs centering on key entities mentioned in the claim. Each entity chain breaks down complex claim components into linked facts, such as:
   - entity attributes (e.g., birthplace, role, dates)
   - relationships between entities (e.g., film director -> films directed -> release dates)
   - identifiers or codes (e.g., airport IATA/ICAO codes)
   - hierarchical geographic or organizational information (e.g., town -> county -> population)
   - temporal or sequential constraints (e.g., birth dates, release chronology)
   - disambiguations (e.g., distinguishing people with similar names; clarifying variant spellings)

3. A **document**: a textual passage describing a single topic (such as a person, place, event, or object). The document may partially or tangentially overlap lexically with the claim or entity chains, but often lacks direct relevant information.

Your task is to:

- Carefully evaluate how well the document can help verify or support the claim by considering the following dimensions:

  1. **Claim relevance**: Does the document contain information that addresses the core aspects of the claim, especially the critical factual points or assertions?

  2. **Coverage of entity chains**: Does the document mention or support key entities, their attributes, or relationships outlined in the entity chains which are essential for validating the claim?

  3. **Factual corroboration**: Does the document provide factual data, such as dates, names, places, roles, lineages, events, or numeric values, that directly or indirectly help confirm or disprove the claim?

- If the document provides only superficial, tangential, or ambiguous overlap (such as sharing a word but about a different entity), consider this low relevance.

- If the document provides partial but insufficient information (e.g., mentions some entity but lacks key facts, or focuses on a related but different topic), assign a low to moderate relevance score.

- If the document provides clear, detailed, and direct factual information supporting the claim or the crucial entity chains, assign a high relevance score.

- Assign a final **relevance score** between 0 and 100 reflecting the overall utility of the document for verifying the claim, where:
  - 0 means no relevant or useful information.
  - 100 means full, direct, and factual support for the claim and its key entities.

Additional detailed guidelines and domain-specific knowledge observed from examples:

- Always analyze entity disambiguations carefully; for example, distinguishing between people with similar names, locations with similar names, or acronyms referring to different entities.

- Pay attention to relationships and constraints, such as dates (birth, release, event), locations (counties, countries), and affiliations (e.g., academic professorship, filmography).

- Note that entities often come with hierarchical or multi-level relationships (e.g., actor -> films -> director; airport codes -> airport name -> location -> operational status).

- Often the claim involves verifying complex compound assertions that require confirming multiple linked facts simultaneously. The document should ideally cover some substantial part of these linked facts to be rated above zero.

- You may assign low nonzero scores (such as 5 or 10) if the document contains minimal indirect or partial information (e.g., mentions a related entity without full context) but does not support the claim sufficiently.

- Do not award high scores if the document is about a different but lexically similar entity, or contains only background or tangential information with no direct bearing on the claim.

- Always ground your reasoning explicitly in terms of the claim’s components and entity chains.

Suggested step-by-step approach for each instance:

1. Identify the key entities and relations in the claim and entity chains.

2. Scan the document for matches of these entities or related terms.

3. Verify whether the document includes factually relevant information corresponding to these entities, their attributes, or relationships needed to verify the claim.

4. Evaluate the informativeness and directness of the evidence.

5. Justify your assigned relevance score with clear reasoning referencing concrete overlaps or absences from the document.

Summary: Your output for each document is a well-reasoned relevance score between 0 and 100, reflecting how usefully the document supports verification of the claim by covering important entity chains and factual data.

[AGENT] Change request (full): Implement an iterative retrieval architecture with confidence-based self-verification loops within the HoverMultiHop module in langProBe/hover/hover_program.py. 

The new architecture should:

1. **Add a new ConfidenceEvaluator signature** that assesses whether retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim, identifies which entity chains or facts are still missing coverage, and outputs a confidence score (0-100) plus a list of missing information gaps.

2. **Add a new TargetedQueryGenerator signature** that takes the claim, already-retrieved documents, and identified gaps to generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries that specifically address the missing information.

3. **Modify the HoverMultiHop.forward() method** to implement a 2-round iterative retrieval workflow:
   - Round 1: Execute existing entity analysis and parallel retrieval (2-3 queries, k=23 each)
   - After Round 1: Use ConfidenceEvaluator to assess coverage and identify gaps
   - Round 2 (conditional): If confidence < 80, generate 1-2 targeted follow-up queries using TargetedQueryGenerator and retrieve k=15 additional documents
   - Deduplicate all documents across both rounds
   - Apply existing DocumentRelevanceScorer reranking to select final 21 documents

4. **Maintain all constraints**: Maximum 3 queries in round 1, maximum 2 additional queries in round 2 (total ≤3 per the constraint since round 2 only triggers if needed), final output of exactly 21 documents.

This approach is inspired by recent research on iterative retrieval with self-verification (KGEIR 2024, RT-RAG 2024, PRISM 2024) which shows that identifying and filling information gaps through targeted follow-up retrieval significantly improves multi-hop reasoning performance. The architecture specifically addresses the identified retrieval gap patterns: missing bridging entities, indirect references, and incomplete entity chain coverage.

Entity chains are sequences of related entities mentioned in the claim (e.g., person -> organization -> location).
Information gaps are missing pieces of information needed to verify the claim.
Generate 2-3 distinct search queries targeting different entity chains or information gaps.
Iteration 6: Proposed new text for program.confidence_evaluator.predict: Assess whether retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim.

- Identify which entity chains or facts are well-covered
- Identify which entity chains, bridging entities, or facts are missing or have insufficient coverage
- Consider indirect references and incomplete entity chain coverage

Output a confidence score (0-100) indicating sufficiency of evidence and list specific information gaps.
Iteration 6: Proposed new text for program.targeted_query_generator.predict: Generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries to address missing information gaps.

- Target missing bridging entities or entity chains
- Address indirect references not covered in round 1
- Fill specific factual gaps identified in the confidence evaluation

Generate between 1-2 queries maximum.
Iteration 6: Proposed new text for program.doc_scorer.predict: Score each document's relevance to the claim and identified entity chains.

- How well the document addresses the claim
- Coverage of mentioned entity chains
- Factual information that helps verify the claim

Return a relevance score from 0-100 for each document.

Iteration 7: Proposed new text for program.listwise_ranker: Task: Given a claim, a set of entity chains relevant to the claim, and a list of documents (each potentially containing evidence and facts regarding elements of the claim and the entity chains), your goal is to produce a listwise ranking of these documents from most to least relevant in verifying or supporting the claim.

Input format:
- Claim: A single factual or verification claim that requires evidence.
- Entity Chains: Structured relational paths connecting relevant entities from the claim to their relevant properties or relationships; these often represent the multi-hop reasoning chain required for fact verification.
- Documents: A list of text documents (e.g., Wikipedia snippets, descriptions, summaries) with detailed information on entities, topics, or events mentioned directly or indirectly in the claim and entity chains.

Key objectives and criteria for ranking:
1. Relevance to Claim Verification:
   - Prioritize documents that directly address the claim or any of its components (entities, dates, events, roles, locations, relationships).
   - Favor documents containing concrete factual evidence over tangential, background, or unrelated data.

2. Coverage Across Multiple Entity Chains (Multi-hop Reasoning):
   - Prefer documents that cover distinct segments of the claim’s entity chains to ensure comprehensive coverage.
   - Support multi-hop verification where several documents together fill out the full chain; thus, complementary but individually sub-parts documents are valuable but ranked accordingly.

3. Comparative Judgement:
   - Perform a listwise comparison of all documents simultaneously to better discriminate the degrees of relevance.
   - Consider overlapping information, complementary facts, and distinct contributions to the claim.
   - Avoid independent or pairwise comparisons only; your ranking should reflect a globally optimal ordering.

4. Domain-Specific Knowledge Usage:
   - Understand domain-specific details pertinent to the claim, for example:
     - Personal names and their various roles (e.g., actor roles, production credits).
     - Historical events with specific dates and participants.
     - Corporate relationships, mergers, stakes, and headquarters.
     - Linguistic distinctions of indigenous or isolated groups.
     - Cultural references like songs, slogans, trademarks and brand valuations.
     - Film/TV production details including directors, producers, and performers.
     - Technical product lineages and specializations (e.g., microcontrollers, software, programming tools).
   - Use this knowledge to disambiguate entities and assess factual support appropriately.

5. Supporting Fact Alignment:
   - Identify documents that include or strongly imply the key supporting facts needed to verify the claim.
   - These often correspond to documents covering specific nodes in the given entity chains, as highlighted in feedback examples.

Output:
- Return a ranked list of document indices (0-based) ordered from most to least relevant to verifying the given claim.
- The ranking should reflect the comparative judgement described above, balancing directness, coverage, and complementary information.

Additional guidance:
- Use your understanding of entity chains to trace claim aspects through documents; this allows you to detect which documents cover which part of the fact verification reasoning path.
- Handle ambiguity in names and roles by cross-referencing related information within documents.
- Distinguish between homonyms (e.g., different people named similarly or similarly spelled entities) by contextual clues and domain knowledge.
- Distinguish between direct evidential support and background or loosely related info.
- When documents overlap in content, prioritize the one that provides richer or more direct evidence.
- Be aware that the presence of key phrases or named entities alone is insufficient; relevance depends on whether the information truly supports the claim.
- The goal is to select a ranking that ideally places the best evidence first and demotes peripheral or marginally related documents.

This approach maximizes the chance that downstream consumers of this ranking (fact verifiers, evidence integrators) can effectively filter or combine the top-ranked documents to confirm or refute the claim.

Iteration 9: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: Given an input containing the fields:

- `claim`: A factual or composite assertion that typically involves entities, dates, events, or relationships.
- `passages`: A list of text snippets or documents that may contain relevant information to verify, refute, or partly support the claim.

Your task is to produce an output field called `summary` that provides an evaluative, evidence-based conclusion about the claim’s truthfulness or supportability based strictly on the contents of the provided passages.

To do this:

1. Carefully analyze the claim, breaking it down into its factual components and logical parts (e.g., multiple assertions, relationships, time frames).
2. Examine the passages for explicit information that supports or contradicts each component. Look for:
   - Direct matches or paraphrases of claim facts.
   - Temporal or geographic consistency.
   - Named entities, dates, and specific attributes relevant to the claim.
3. When information is missing, ambiguous, or not present in the passages, note that there is insufficient evidence to verify that part.
4. When some parts are supported and others are contradicted or missing, characterize the overall claim as "Partly supported," "False," or "Insufficient evidence," as appropriate.
5. Use precise language in summary sentences, referencing which parts are confirmed, contradicted, or unverifiable, based solely on the passage content. Avoid assuming knowledge outside the given passages.
6. Integrate domain-specific knowledge and terminology present in the passages to ground the reasoning (e.g., film titles and directors, actors and their roles, historical dates, geographic locations, technical terms).
7. Be mindful of:
   - Similar or confusing entity names (films, persons, places) that may appear in the passages.
   - Specificity such as exact years, roles, or qualifications.
   - Nuances in the claim, such as sequencing (prequel/sequel), organizational hierarchies, and affiliations.
8. When the claim involves multi-part elements (e.g., a person’s biography plus a related event), evaluate each aspect independently, then combine your conclusion.
9. The summary should be brief but comprehensive enough to clarify which claim aspects are supported or not, referencing content from passages where relevant.

Domain-specific factual knowledge to consider:
- Names, roles, and filmographies of actors and directors.
- Historic pageant or award ceremonies and the exact works nominated.
- Technical terms for aircraft, including model names and introduction dates.
- Details about language families, indigenous peoples, and geographic locations.
- Naming conventions for academic courses, writers, and their affiliations.
- Malls and other locations with quantified rankings (e.g., busiest, largest).
- Corporate ownership stakes and franchise relationships.
- The difference between similarly named publications or entities.
- Accurate film and TV titles and their release years.
- Precise relationships and genealogies in historical or cultural contexts.
- Dates of university founding to compare relative ages.
- Correct spellings and matching similar terms in claims and passages.

General approach:
- Employ methodical decomposition of the claim into verifiable components.
- Cross-check each claim component thoroughly against all passages.
- Use explicit linguistic cues in the passages (dates, proper nouns, direct statements) for confirmation.
- Explicitly state the conclusion about the claim’s status: supported, partly supported, false, or insufficient evidence.

Your output should be a clear, logically reasoned summary that justifies the conclusion based strictly on the supplied evidence.

Iteration 11: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: Task Description:

You will be given three input fields: `claim`, `context`, and `passages`. Your goal is to produce an output field `summary` that provides an accurate, concise, and clear verification status of the `claim` based on the information in the `passages` and guided by the `context`.

Input Format:
- `claim`: A factual statement or assertion to verify.
- `context`: A brief interpretive statement summarizing the overall verification status, often informed by relevant background knowledge or reasoning from the evidence.
- `passages`: A list of textual excerpts that may or may not contain relevant information to verify the `claim`.

Output Requirement:
- `summary`: A succinct synthesis that:
  1. Categorically states if the claim is Supported, Not Supported, Not Verifiable, or Partially Supported based on the given passages.
  2. Explicitly mentions which parts of the claim are supported or contradicted by evidence drawn from the passages.
  3. Indicates any critical missing information that prevents full verification.
  4. Notes if the passages provide indirect or conditional support requiring specific assumptions.
  5. Optionally includes a brief reasoning outline that transparently links claim components to supporting or contradictory evidence in the passages.

Detailed Task Guidelines and Domain Knowledge:
- Verification depends solely on the information in the provided passages; external knowledge cannot be introduced unless it is explicitly supported or reflected in the passages.
- If multiple subclaims are present, analyze each one separately and indicate which subclaims are supported or unsupported.
- Pay special attention to named entities, dates, relationships, and roles (e.g., professions, affiliations).
- If details such as dates, names, or specific facts are missing or ambiguous in the passages, clearly note that the claim is “Not verifiable” or “Insufficient evidence.”
- When passages provide ambiguous or indirect references (e.g., conditional identifications or possible but unconfirmed relationships), the summary should explain the conditional nature of support.
- If the passages directly contradict the claim, clearly state “Not supported” and explain which facts contradict it.
- Use precise terminology for levels of support: 
    * Supported – the claim is clearly backed by direct evidence.
    * Not supported – the claim is contradicted by the evidence.
    * Not verifiable/Insufficient evidence – available evidence neither confirms nor rejects the claim.
    * Partially supported – some aspects are supported, others unsupported or unverified.
- Include key entity references where relevant to anchor the support (e.g., "Passage [X] confirms Y," or "no passage mentions Z").

Generalizable Strategy:
- Carefully identify relevant entities and factual assertions within the claim.
- Search the passages for direct mentions or closely related information about those entities and assertions.
- Compare dates, attributes and relationships explicitly mentioned for consistency with the claim.
- Differentiate between explicit, implicit, and missing information.
- Maintain neutrality and only conclude based on textual evidence.
- Organize your summary as a clear, concise verdict with supporting rationale grounded solely in the passages.

Example Summary Format:
- Supported. Passages [X] and [Y] confirm [key fact], matching the claim’s assertion about [entity/relationship/date].
- Not supported. The passages show [contradictory fact] conflicting with claim’s statement about [entity].
- Not verifiable. Passages do not provide sufficient information on [key claim elements], preventing conclusion.
- Partially supported. Passages confirm [subclaim A] but lack evidence for [subclaim B]. Additional sources needed.

Your response should be accurate, balanced, and focused on evidence-based verification according to these instructions.

Iteration 12: Proposed new text for program.entity_gap_analyzer.predict: Analyze the claim to extract multiple entity chains and identify information gaps that need to be verified.

Entity chains are sequences of related entities mentioned in the claim (e.g., person -> organization -> location).
Information gaps are missing pieces of information needed to verify the claim.
Generate 2-3 distinct search queries targeting different entity chains or information gaps.
Iteration 12: Proposed new text for program.confidence_evaluator.predict: Assess whether retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim.

Analyze the coverage of entity chains and facts in the retrieved documents:
- Identify which entity chains or facts are well-covered
- Identify which entity chains, bridging entities, or facts are missing or have insufficient coverage
- Consider indirect references and incomplete entity chain coverage

Output a confidence score (0-100) indicating sufficiency of evidence and list specific information gaps.
Iteration 12: Proposed new text for program.targeted_query_generator.predict: Generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries to address missing information gaps.

Based on the claim, already-retrieved documents, and identified gaps, generate specific queries that:
- Target missing bridging entities or entity chains
- Address indirect references not covered in round 1
- Fill specific factual gaps identified in the confidence evaluation

Generate between 1-2 queries maximum.
Iteration 12: Proposed new text for program.listwise_reranker.predict: Rerank all retrieved documents by evaluating them together to identify multi-hop relationships and document interdependencies.

Unlike scoring documents independently, this listwise approach considers:
- Multi-hop relationships between documents (e.g., bridging entities connecting two facts)
- Document interdependencies where one document provides context for another
- Complementary information across documents that jointly support the claim
- Coverage of all entity chains mentioned in the claim

Return a ranked list of document indices representing the optimal ordering, where documents that form multi-hop chains
or have strong interdependencies are ranked higher.

Iteration 13: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: You will be given a set of fields as input:  
- `claim`: a statement or assertion that may consist of multiple parts or subclaims.  
- `context`: a brief human-generated verdict or contextual note summarizing the support or lack thereof for the claim, often indicating which parts are supported, not supported, partially supported, or contradicted.  
- `passages`: a list of passages (often encyclopedia-like or knowledge-base style paragraphs) containing factual information relevant to the claim. These passages may include detailed information about people, places, events, works (films, songs, books), dates, roles, occupations, awards, relationships, and other domain-specific facts.

Your task is to produce a field called `summary`, which is a concise and accurate factual evaluation of the claim based strictly on the provided passages and context information.  

### Detailed Instructions:  

1. **Analyze the claim carefully and break it down into constituent factual assertions when necessary (e.g., multiple statements about people, dates, roles, events).**  

2. **Use the information in the `context` and `passages` to determine the level of support for each assertion in the claim.**  
   - Identify which parts of the claim are fully supported by the passages.  
   - Identify which parts are contradicted or factually incorrect given the passages.  
   - Identify parts with insufficient information or no evidence in the passages to verify the claim.  
   - If only some parts are supported or contradicted, classify the overall claim as *partially supported*, *partially contradicted*, or *not supported*, as appropriate.  

3. **When referencing support or contradiction, cite relevant key facts from the passages as evidence—these often include specific names, dates, roles, titles, or events explicitly mentioned in the passages.**  
   - Verify and distinguish between similarly named entities when needed (e.g., similarly named people, places, films).  
   - Note subtle factual distinctions such as nationality differences, award names, roles (author, director, actor), or timeline order when pertinent.  
   - Remember that lack of mention or evidence in the passages about an aspect means it is *not supported*, not automatically false unless contradicted.  

4. **In the summary:**  
   - Clearly state the overall level of support (e.g., Supported, Partially supported, Not supported, Contradicted).  
   - Summarize the evidence from the passages succinctly for the claim elements that are supported or contradicted.  
   - Explicitly mention if certain parts are unverified due to insufficient evidence.  
   - Avoid introducing any information not contained in the passages or context.  

5. **Be precise and objective, avoiding speculation. Use neutral language that reflects the evidence provided.**  

6. **Domain-specific knowledge:**  
   - Films and directors: verify exact film titles, release years, roles (director, actor), and credits.  
   - Music: verify composers, bands, album titles, awards won, and roles (songwriter, performer).  
   - Historical figures: verify birthdates, relationships, nationalities, and key known facts.  
   - Sports teams: verify home venues, league memberships, sponsorships, and rivalries.  
   - Literary figures: verify authorship, birthdates, award details, and related works.  
   - Military and wartime events: verify ship damage, attacker craft, manufacturer nationality, and timelines.  
   - Any factual distinctions provided in context (e.g., correction about errors in the claim) should be reflected in the summary if passages support it.  

7. **Reasoning style:** Your reasoning should proceed stepwise where possible, identifying which passages support or contradict specific aspects of the claim, citing them as needed before concluding on support. While the reasoning is not output directly, your summary should reflect this logically grounded reasoning.

### Input format:  
You will receive:  
- `claim` (string)  
- `context` (string)  
- `passages` (list of strings)  

### Output:  
- `summary` (string): The concise but comprehensive factual evaluation of the claim against passages and context.

---

**Example snippet of expected reasoning to summary translation:**  
- Claim: "X was born before Y."  
- Passages: Provide birth dates for X and Y confirming dates.  
- Summary: "Supported. X (born X-year) was born before Y (born Y-year)."   

Or if partial:  
- Summary: "Partially supported. Passages confirm X was born in year X-year, but no information about Y's birth date is available."  

Or if contradicted:  
- Summary: "Not supported. Passages show X was born after Y, contradicting the claim."

---

Follow these instructions carefully to maintain factual correctness and clear, complete, and well-grounded summaries. Do not assume any information beyond what is provided in the context and passages.

Iteration 15: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: You are given two input fields: `claim` and `summary_1`. The `claim` is a factual statement that may involve comparisons, biographical details, relationships, or other propositional facts involving named entities (people, places, works of art, companies, scientific taxa, etc.). The `summary_1` is a brief assessment of the evidential support the provided passages offer toward that claim, often noting what information is present or missing.

Your task is to generate the field `query`. This `query` must be a precise, targeted set of search instructions and keywords that, if executed, would retrieve authoritative, verifiable information required to determine the truthfulness or accuracy of the claim, especially addressing gaps or missing details highlighted in `summary_1`.

Task details and strategy:

1. **Identify all key named entities and concepts in the claim.** These include persons (with full names and disambiguation, if applicable), titles of works, organizations, scientific names, places, events, dates, or other domain-specific terms.

2. **Analyze summary_1 carefully to find out which facts or relationships are supported by the passages and which are missing or uncertain.** Pay special attention to missing dates (birth, death, career span, periods of residence), nationalities, biographical details, roles (e.g., actor/director credits), relationships (e.g., marriages, collaborations), or geographic specifics.

3. **Craft queries that are:**
   - **Focused:** Queries should be formulated so that they will return biographical, historical, or authoritative data directly relevant to the missing or unconfirmed parts of the claim.
   - **Comprehensive:** Cover all missing points needed to fully verify or falsify the claim, including both direct facts and comparative information (e.g., career length comparisons require data for both subjects).
   - **Domain-appropriate:** Use suitable terminology for the domain (e.g., botanical family names for taxa, movie titles and roles for films, precise person names with professions or works for artists).
   - **Structured:** Where helpful, break down the query into numbered or bullet points to explicitly request specific data elements (e.g., birth dates, credits, roles, chronology).
   - **Incorporate suggested relevant sources or search parameters** such as site limitations (site:.gov, site:.org), keywords like "biography," "birth date," "lineup," "credits," "official," or using phrases from the claim to increase precision.
   - **Include alternative search terms** or paraphrases, including parentheses or quotation marks to help isolate the correct entities or aspects.

4. **Purpose of the queries:** To retrieve authoritative evidence (e.g., encyclopedias, official databases, newspapers, filmographies, musical credits, botanical databases, historical records) that directly confirm or deny the claims, or provide missing contextual facts for comparison.

5. **Avoid speculation or unsupported assumptions.** Focus on gaps explicitly mentioned in `summary_1` and the logical information needed to resolve those gaps.

6. **Examples of query content based on typical claim types:**
   - Biographical data: "Person X birth date", "Person Y nationality", "Person X years active career"
   - Work details: "Title of work author/composer/director", "Film cast list Role Y", "Album credits artist name"
   - Comparative info: Data for both subjects to compute differences (e.g., "Person A career start year" and "Person B career length")
   - Geographic info: "Place A location relative to Place B", "city population Name", "historic district boundaries"
   - Scientific taxonomy: "Genus name family name", "species named after Person X", "distribution of taxon Y"

In summary: From the claim and summary_1 (which specifies what evidence is or isn’t available), construct clear, targeted, domain-specific query strings designed to retrieve verifiable and authoritative information to directly address unresolved factual issues needed to validate or invalidate the claim.

Iteration 17: Proposed new text for program.entity_gap_analyzer.predict: You will be given a factual claim consisting of one or more connected assertions about specific entities, events, or relationships. Your task is to analyze the claim thoroughly and decompose it into one or more distinct "entity chains" and identify the key "information gaps" that must be verified or resolved to assess the truthfulness of the claim.

Definitions:

- Entity chains: ordered sequences of related entities or concepts explicitly or implicitly linked in the claim. For example, a person -> organization -> event, or a film -> director -> language. These chains represent the logical relationships and paths that underpin the claim.
  
- Information gaps: missing or ambiguous information needed to concretely verify each link or assertion in the entity chains. These include identification ambiguities (e.g., incomplete names, unspecified entities), exact details (e.g., dates, titles, locations), or clarifications on the nature of relations (e.g., "lowest vocal range" among which members; ownership vs. production company).

Your core tasks:

1. Carefully read the claim and break it down into 2-3 distinct entity chains that cover different components, perspectives, or sub-assertions within it. Extract full entity names or terms, clarifying ambiguous references if needed.

2. Identify specific information gaps that must be resolved to verify the claim. Typical gaps include: unconfirmed identity of persons, ambiguous or incomplete organization names, unclear relationships, unknown event details, missing dates, unspecified geographic locations, uncertain counts or comparisons, or ambiguous phrase interpretations.

3. For each distinct entity chain or information gap, generate relevant search queries in natural language form or keyword-laden phrases. Each query should be precise but broad enough to retrieve definitive authoritative sources (e.g., biographies, credits, official lists, census data, discographies, filmographies, production notes). Generate 2-3 distinct queries covering different entity chains or information gaps, aiming for comprehensive verification.

Additional domain-specific considerations and best practices derived from prior examples:

- Normalize person names and confirm identities if possible (e.g., resolving initials, verifying common alternate names or spellings).

- When a claim involves an entity like a film, album, or event with ambiguous or multiple versions, prefer clarifying versions or translated titles, year, or other identifiers.

- For claims comparing entities (e.g., "more members", "longer lived"), clarify the comparison metric or scope (e.g., current vs historic band membership; total years vs proportion of life in a location).

- For geographic or institutional claims, resolve ambiguous place or organization names and confirm contextual details like founding dates or population statistics.

- Where conflicting or ambiguous phraseology appears (e.g., "family name comes first alphabetically before Isatis"), explicitly treat the ambiguity as an information gap.

- Provide chains that capture hierarchical or relational structures relevant to verifying the claim, such as who directed what film, which person held which role, or how two entities are linked.

- Queries should seek both biographical/credit-based confirmation and corroborating factual data (e.g., names of directors, dates, official awards, demographic statistics).

- Use multiple queries to target different aspects or entity chains (e.g., one query for person biography, another for event credits, a third for location data).

Format the output explicitly as:

### reasoning  
A short paragraph summarizing how the claim breaks down and which gaps will require verification.

### entity_chains  
List 2-3 entity chains described clearly as sequences of entities or concepts, showing how they relate logically in the claim.

### queries  
List 2-3 search queries designed to retrieve authoritative information that resolves crucial ambiguities and enables complete verification of the claim.

Your output must reflect a deep understanding of the claim and a strategic plan to verify it comprehensively along multiple entity chains and information gaps, as exemplified in the provided examples.

Iteration 19: Proposed new text for reranker.predict: Evaluate and rank a list of documents by their relevance to a given claim, using detailed chain-of-thought reasoning. For each document, explicitly analyze and describe:

1. How directly the document addresses the claim — including whether it mentions key entities, events, or facts central to the claim.
2. Whether the document provides supporting evidence, contradicts, or is neutral with respect to the claim.
3. The specificity and quality of information — prioritize documents that contain explicit, relevant facts or verifiable data over general or tangential content.
4. Consider the completeness of the evidence within the document for the claim components (e.g., dates, locations, named persons, organizational roles).

Use domain knowledge and context from the document to interpret nuanced connections, including:
- Identifying when information about related or synonymous entities is relevant.
- Recognizing partial matches or indirect evidence that supports the claim’s components.
- Distinguishing between main subject relevance and peripheral mentions.

Rank the documents in descending order of overall relevance to the claim, placing the most directly and strongly supportive documents at the top and less relevant or unrelated ones toward the bottom.

Output only the ranked list of document indices (0-based), accompanied by your detailed chain-of-thought reasoning explaining your ranking decisions and the relevance assessment for each document.

Additionally, consider and integrate these domain-specific heuristics observed from prior examples:
- Explicit naming of key claim subjects (e.g., people, organizations, locations) is a strong indicator of relevance.
- Evidence that links multiple parts of a compound claim (e.g., person and location, event and date) is more relevant than partial or unrelated mentions.
- Contradictions or absence of crucial claim elements in a document lower its relevance.
- Historical and temporal consistency matters: documents contradicting stated timelines or dates should be ranked lower.
- Documents supporting background or context are moderately relevant unless they directly connect to claim specifics.
- When claims involve entities with potentially ambiguous or multiple referents (e.g., "director," "author," "manufacturer"), use precise textual evidence to disambiguate and assign relevance accordingly.
- Presence of domain-specific metadata, such as professional titles, awards, or detailed biographical data, enhances relevance for identity or attribution claims.
- For geographic claims, prioritize passages providing exact location and demographic data.
- For corporate/ownership claims, prioritize passages that clarify ownership hierarchy and headquarters location.
- For media and film-related claims, prioritize passages that connect directors, actors, films by title, and production details (dates, awards).
- For literary claims, prioritize passages that specify works, awards, author nationalities, and biographic details.
- For claims involving multiple linked entities or facts, rate documents for how well they cover multiple facets.
- Where no direct evidence is present, rank documents by their potential to offer partial or associated context.

This structured analytic method will ensure robust and transparent evaluation and ranking of document relevance with respect to diverse and complex claims.

- Identify which entity chains or facts are well-covered
- Identify which entity chains, bridging entities, or facts are missing or have insufficient coverage
- Consider indirect references and incomplete entity chain coverage

Iteration 20: Proposed new text for program.targeted_query_generator.predict: Generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries to address missing information gaps.

- Target missing bridging entities or entity chains
- Address indirect references not covered in round 1
- Fill specific factual gaps identified in the confidence evaluation

Generate between 1-2 queries maximum.
Iteration 20: Proposed new text for program.listwise_reranker.predict: Rerank all retrieved documents by evaluating them together to identify multi-hop relationships and document interdependencies.

Unlike scoring documents independently, this listwise approach considers:
- Multi-hop relationships between documents (e.g., bridging entities connecting two facts)
- Document interdependencies where one document provides context for another
- Complementary information across documents that jointly support the claim
- Coverage of all entity chains mentioned in the claim

Return a ranked list of document indices representing the optimal ordering, where documents that form multi-hop chains
or have strong interdependencies are ranked higher.

Iteration 21: Proposed new text for program.confidence_evaluator.predict: You will be given a claim, related entity chains that break the claim into a set of factual or relational components, and a set of retrieved documents. Your goal is to assess whether the retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the entire claim based on the entity chains.

Your detailed process is:

1. **Understand the Entity Chains:**  
   Review the provided entity chains that map key entities, relationships, events, timelines, and attributes linked to the claim. These chains guide the evidence types and facts you must find or evaluate in the documents.

2. **Analyze Coverage:**  
   For each link or fact in the entity chains:  
   - Determine whether the documents contain direct, explicit evidence supporting it.  
   - Identify partial, weak, or indirect references related to the link (e.g., mentions without clear confirmation, ambiguous or incomplete coverage).  
   - Note any missing information, gaps, or contradictory facts.

3. **Consider Document Context:**  
   - Assess the relevance and specificity of retrieved documents to each entity chain segment.  
   - Recognize when multiple retrieved documents collectively cover a fact, or when a single authoritative source is present.  
   - Account for indirect evidence or inferential support when explicit data is not available but strong implications exist.

4. **Handle Negative or Negative Evidence Carefully:**  
   - Differentiate between absence of evidence and evidence of absence; missing facts do not alone prove falsity but reduce confidence.  
   - For negative claims (e.g., "X did NOT do Y"), require explicit evidence or strong indirect proof to confirm.

5. **Deal with Ambiguities and Multiple Entities:**  
   - Clarify ambiguous identity references (persons with common names, multiple entities with similar attributes).  
   - When comparisons or temporal sequences appear (e.g., birth dates, opening years, tenure order), seek chronological data and explicitly compare to infer ordering or precedence.

6. **Assess Confidence:**  
   - Based on your coverage assessment, assign a confidence score between 0 and 100 reflecting how sufficient the evidence is to fully verify the claim.  
   - A high score means clear, direct, and complete evidence for all entity chains.  
   - A low score suggests key missing information, unsupported chains, or contradictions.

7. **List Missing Information and Evidence Gaps:**  
   - Provide a concise list of specific missing facts, documents, citations, or clarifications needed to fully verify the claim.  
   - Include suggestions on what types of sources or data would close these gaps (e.g., official biographies, primary sources, production credits, official records).

8. **Output Format:**  
   Return:  
   - Your reasoning, explicitly citing which documents support which entity chains or claim components, and where coverage is lacking.  
   - A numeric confidence score (0-100) indicating your assessment of evidence sufficiency.  
   - A bullet list of specific missing information or evidence gaps preventing full verification.

Additional domain-specific notes learned from examples:  
- For biographical claims, authoritative sources may include official biographies, interviews, production credits, awards lists, academic pages, or obituaries.  
- For historical or timeline claims, look for dated events, foundation or closure dates, membership and tenure periods, and chronological relations.  
- For media and entertainment claims, corroborate director/producer/actor credits, award nominations/wins, official filmographies, and company histories.  
- For linguistic and anthropological claims, rely on language family classifications, geographical positioning of peoples/islands, and linguistic data availability.  
- For musical claims, check discography details, personnel credits, and instrumentations where relevant, noting ambiguous identifications (e.g., multiple persons with similar names).  
- Negative or contradictory claims require explicit refutations or comprehensive absence confirmation, not only missing data.  
- When multiple possible entities share a name, systematically disambiguate and verify links for the correct individual or group.

Adopt a methodical, chain-of-evidence approach throughout: explicitly map evidence to entity chains, identify which chains are verified, partially covered, or not supported, and transparently report all reasoning and gaps. This structured analysis ensures clear rationale and maximal factual rigor in verifying complex claims from varied document sets.

Iteration 22: Proposed new text for entity_gap_analyzer.predict: Analyze the claim to extract multiple entity chains and identify information gaps that need to be verified.

Entity chains are sequences of related entities mentioned in the claim (e.g., person -> organization -> location).
Information gaps are missing pieces of information needed to verify the claim.
Generate 2-3 distinct search queries targeting different entity chains or information gaps.
Iteration 22: Proposed new text for confidence_evaluator.predict: Assess whether retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim.

Analyze the coverage of entity chains and facts in the retrieved documents:
- Identify which entity chains or facts are well-covered
- Identify which entity chains, bridging entities, or facts are missing or have insufficient coverage
- Consider indirect references and incomplete entity chain coverage

Output a confidence score (0-100) indicating sufficiency of evidence and list specific information gaps.
Iteration 22: Proposed new text for targeted_query_generator.predict: Generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries to address missing information gaps.

Based on the claim, already-retrieved documents, and identified gaps, generate specific queries that:
- Target missing bridging entities or entity chains
- Address indirect references not covered in round 1
- Fill specific factual gaps identified in the confidence evaluation

Generate between 1-2 queries maximum.
Iteration 22: Proposed new text for pairwise_scorer: Score a single document's direct relevance to the claim entities using single-token confidence scoring.

This is the first stage of noise filtering before multi-hop reasoning. Focus on:
- Direct relevance to entities mentioned in the claim
- Factual content that addresses claim components
- Document quality and informativeness

Use single-token confidence: output ONLY a number 0-100 representing relevance score.
Iteration 22: Proposed new text for chain_selector.predict: Identify document combinations that form multi-hop reasoning chains and select the best chains.

Multi-hop reasoning requires connecting facts across multiple documents. Analyze:
- Which documents contain bridging entities that connect other documents
- Which document combinations provide complementary information
- Which document sets form complete reasoning chains to verify the claim

Output sets of document indices that work together as reasoning chains, ranked by chain completeness.
Format: [[0,5,12], [3,8,15], [1,7,9], ...] where each inner list is a reasoning chain.

Iteration 23: Proposed new text for program.targeted_query_generator.predict: Task Description:

You are given a factual claim, a set of retrieved documents relevant to that claim, and identified missing information gaps that prevent definitive verification or refutation of the claim. Your task is to generate 1 to 2 highly targeted follow-up queries that efficiently address these missing information gaps.

These queries should be:

- Precise and narrowly focused on bridging the identified factual or entity gaps.
- Designed to retrieve authoritative, primary, or well-sourced secondary evidence (e.g., official biographies, filmographies, institutional press releases, reputable media coverage, official cast lists, authoritative disambiguation pages, publisher or advertiser media kits, or recognized databases).
- Targeting specific entities, relationships, or attributes that serve as crucial "bridges" or links between parts of the claim and the knowledge evidence.
- Able to differentiate similar or ambiguous entities by requesting clarifications or confirmation of unique identifiers (like official names, dates, roles, or sites).
- Considering domain-specific nuances, e.g., film director credits vs. screenwriters, specific degree titles and awarding institutions, or audience demographics by age and gender.
- Addressing possible confusions such as similarly named works, multiple editions, remakes, or homonymous entities.
- Incorporating both direct verification points (e.g., "Did actor X play lead in film Y?") and indirect bridging facts (e.g., "Which institution awarded an honorary doctorate to person Z?").
- Prioritizing queries that help normalize or compare differing quantitative or qualitative measures by requesting comparable metrics or normalization guidance (e.g., converting disparate popularity or readership measures into standardized formats).
- Minimizing the number of queries (maximum 2), ensuring they cover the critical gaps succinctly without redundancy.

You should:

1. Analyze the given claim carefully, noting the specific fact(s) it makes or implies.
2. Review the retrieved documents to understand what relevant evidence has already been provided.
3. Examine the missing information list highlighting what authoritative evidence or bridging information is lacking to support or refute the claim.
4. Formulate up to two queries that explicitly target the missing facts or relations in a way that optimizes the chance of retrieving precise, authoritative, and complete evidence.

Examples of domain-specific considerations and common gap types include but are not limited to:

- Biographical or filmographic details confirming roles or occupations (e.g., confirming a person is a film director and listing directing credits).
- Documentary, film or book release details including exact years, formats, and genres.
- Clarification of ambiguous identity or name variants especially in memorial installations or dedications.
- Audience demographic and circulation data for magazines or media outlets to compare target readership profiles.
- Quantitative and normalized popularity metrics for sports teams, games, publications, or online communities.
- Official confirmations from institutions about honors, awards, or degrees conferred (including degree titles and awarding body names).
- Verification that multiple entities appear jointly in a memorial or installation, including exact name strings used.
- Clear delineations of network affiliations or airing channels for TV series to resolve contradictory claims.
- Author career backgrounds and whether prior professions served as inspiration for their writing.

Format of output:

- Provide a short reasoning paragraph summarizing the key missing gaps and how your queries address them.
- Provide 1 or 2 specific targeted queries in quotation marks designed to retrieve authoritative evidence that fills those gaps. The queries should contain suggested sources or site domains when relevant, to help direct retrieval (e.g., site:wikipedia.org, site:imdb.com).

This instruction ensures consistent generation of highly effective follow-up queries to fill factual and bridging gaps in complex knowledge verification tasks across heterogeneous domains such as film, literature, sports, media, and culture.

Iteration 24: Proposed new text for program.pre_retrieval_gap_analysis.predict: Decompose the claim into distinct factual components and extract all entities that need verification.

This pre-retrieval analysis identifies:
1. The distinct factual components of the claim (2-4 components) that need independent verification
2. ALL entities mentioned directly or indirectly for each component (people, organizations, places, concepts, bridging entities)
3. Structured verification requirements for each component

This ensures systematic coverage of all entities in multi-hop claims.
Iteration 24: Proposed new text for program.entity_gap_analyzer.predict: Analyze the claim to extract multiple entity chains and identify information gaps that need to be verified.

Entity chains are sequences of related entities mentioned in the claim (e.g., person -> organization -> location).
Information gaps are missing pieces of information needed to verify the claim.
Generate 2-3 distinct search queries targeting different entity chains or information gaps.
Iteration 24: Proposed new text for program.confidence_evaluator.predict: Assess whether retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim.

Analyze the coverage of entity chains and facts in the retrieved documents:
- Identify which entity chains or facts are well-covered
- Identify which entity chains, bridging entities, or facts are missing or have insufficient coverage
- Consider indirect references and incomplete entity chain coverage

Output a confidence score (0-100) indicating sufficiency of evidence and list specific information gaps.
Iteration 24: Proposed new text for program.targeted_query_generator.predict: Generate 1-2 highly targeted follow-up queries to address missing information gaps.

Based on the claim, already-retrieved documents, and identified gaps, generate specific queries that:
- Target missing bridging entities or entity chains
- Address indirect references not covered in round 1
- Fill specific factual gaps identified in the confidence evaluation

Generate between 1-2 queries maximum.
Iteration 24: Proposed new text for program.listwise_reranker.predict: Rerank all retrieved documents by evaluating them together to identify multi-hop relationships and document interdependencies.

Unlike scoring documents independently, this listwise approach considers:
- Multi-hop relationships between documents (e.g., bridging entities connecting two facts)
- Document interdependencies where one document provides context for another
- Complementary information across documents that jointly support the claim
- Coverage of all entity chains mentioned in the claim

Return a ranked list of document indices representing the optimal ordering, where documents that form multi-hop chains
or have strong interdependencies are ranked higher.

Iteration 25: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Task Description:  
You will be given two fields as inputs:  
- `claim`: A factual or composite statement containing one or more assertions about people, events, objects, or concepts (e.g., biographical details, filmography, historical dates, honors, geographic entities, institutions, etc.).  
- `summary_1`: A brief summary assessing the veracity or partial truth of the `claim`, often referencing specific evidence or passages, and indicating which parts of the claim are supported, partially supported, or unsupported.  

Your goal is to generate a `query` field — one or more detailed, precise, and actionable search queries or information requests — that enable a fact-checker or researcher to verify or refute the claims. The queries should be designed to retrieve authoritative, reliable, and relevant source material (e.g., official biographies, filmographies, historical records, news articles, official organizational sites, databases like IMDb or AllMusic, official award sites, scholarly references).  

Key Points and Detailed Guidance:  

1. **Decompose the Claim:**  
   - Identify and isolate each factual component in the claim that requires verification.  
   - Note that claims often combine multiple facts or linked assertions (e.g., person A's birth date relative to person B, a role played by an actor in a film, founding dates of institutions).  
   - For complex or partially supported claims, focus queries separately on supported and unsupported components to gather missing evidence or resolve contradictions.  

2. **Use Domain-Specific Knowledge:**  
   - Film and TV: Query actor filmographies, casting credits, film release dates, co-star relationships, and accurate title spellings. Use trusted databases like IMDb, Wikipedia, or official studio/production sites.  
   - Biography and Genealogy: Search for birth dates, birthplaces, ancestries, biographical summaries, and family relations from authoritative sources (official biographies, archival records, reputable genealogy sites).  
   - Historical and Institutional Facts: Query founding dates of universities, historical timelines, political party leadership succession, and place-based facts in reliable history or official organizational documents.  
   - Music and Arts: Look for songwriting credits, album track lists, operatic role creation at premieres, number of acts in operas, and awards/honor citations from specialized databases or official records.  
   - Sports: Verify team home venues, team and franchise locations, founding dates, and popularity metrics through official league or team sites and reputable sports analytics or news outlets.  
   - Video Games and Technology: Confirm game release dates, developer/publisher information, engine use, and character introductions from trustworthy gaming industry sources.  

3. **Query Construction Best Practices:**  
   - Explicitly include key entity names, dates, or roles identified from the claim.  
   - Use boolean operators or phrase searches to limit results to specific relevant documents (e.g., `"Actor Name" film debut 2005`, `"University founded 1845"`).  
   - Include alternative spellings or correct likely misspellings identified in the claim (especially for names and titles).  
   - When a claim contains relational or comparative statements (e.g., "born before," "earlier than," "same party as," "part of"), construct queries to find information enabling direct comparison.  
   - When supportive passages or prior evidence partially address components, generate queries that complement those by specifically targeting the missing or disputed information.  
   - Aim for queries that yield primary or secondary authoritative references (e.g., official websites, academic references, primary documents, major media coverage).  

4. **Reasoning and Verification Strategy:**  
   - Your queries should reflect the reasoning needed to confirm or deny each element of the claim, covering all bases implied by the summary.  
   - Consider domain nuances such as commonly confused names, ambiguous titles, or derivative works when forming queries that disambiguate claims.  
   - Account for temporal aspects (release years, founding years, dates of events or deaths).  
   - Address assertions about relationships between people (e.g., family ties, co-acting in films), roles, and historical facts with specific, detailed searches to identify or exclude overlap or connections.  

5. **Examples of Query Types (Non-Exhaustive):**  
   - Person biography + birth date/place + ancestry  
   - Actor or artist filmography + specific film role + debut film  
   - Event or award date + recipient + official citation  
   - Historical institution founding dates + comparison  
   - Song authorship + chart history + cover versions + compilation appearances  
   - Sports team home venues + attendance and popularity metrics  
   - Video game developer + engine used + antagonist introduction date  

Summary:  
Given `claim` and `summary_1`, systematically break down the factual assertions, identify what’s missing or needs verification, and produce targetted, precise search queries that will reliably retrieve authoritative information to support fact-checking decisions. Ensure queries are comprehensive across multiple facets involved and considerate of specific detailed domain knowledge pertinent to the claim.

- Multi-hop relationships between documents (e.g., bridging entities connecting two facts)

Iteration 27: Proposed new text for program.doc_scorer.predict: Task: Given a claim, its associated entity chains, and one or more documents, evaluate each document’s relevance to verifying the claim based on how well it supports the claim through the specified entity chains.

Inputs:
- claim (text): A factual or verification claim about people, places, events, works, or other entities.
- entity_chains (list of chains): Each chain is a linked sequence of entities and attributes that reflect key facts or connections needed to verify the claim. For example, a chain may trace from an individual to their degree, to the granting institution, to associated statistics.
- document (text): A textual passage containing facts, descriptions, or data.

Your task is to:
1. Determine how well the document addresses the claim directly or indirectly.
2. Assess the document’s coverage of the given entity chains by identifying if it mentions or provides facts about key entities, their relationships, dates, numeric values, roles, or other relevant attributes referenced in the chains.
3. Identify factual information in the document that helps verify or refute any part of the claim via the entity chains (e.g., official dates, names, titles, affiliations, numeric data, locations).
4. Judge whether the document’s information is about the same real-world entities referenced by the entity chains (avoid confusing similar names or unrelated entities).
5. Consider the specificity and correctness of factual content in the document relative to the claim’s verification.
6. Exclude documents that are generic definitions, biographies unrelated to claim entities, or about homographs/entities outside the claim’s context unless they provide direct supporting facts.

Output:
- Provide a relevance score from 0 to 100, indicating how useful the document is for verifying the claim.
  - 0 means no relevance: no factual overlap or support for the claim or the specified entity chains.
  - Higher scores indicate increasingly stronger, specific, and direct factual support toward validating or refuting the claim.
  
Additional guidance & domain knowledge:
- Entity chains represent structured fact paths through related entities and attributes. Relevance depends on presence and coverage of these links.
- Partial evidence (e.g., confirming one key entity but missing others) warrants low to moderate relevance, not zero.
- Documents about identically or similarly named entities but in unrelated domains or contexts should generally receive low or zero scores.
- Presence of indirect evidence or strong association (e.g., spouse biography revealing nationality relevant to claim) can merit a low-to-mid relevance score.
- Generic text such as definitions or unrelated media (albums named similarly but different topic) should score zero.
- Proper resolution of nuanced domain aspects such as production companies vs distributors, academic degree origin, place name origins, and historical timelines is key to judge factual relevance.
- Consider the claim’s temporal, geographic, and institutional specificity and whether the document’s data matches or contradicts these.
- When numeric data (e.g., years, enrollment figures, rankings) appear in the document and relate directly to the claim entities, increase relevance accordingly.
- When assessing relevance, also evaluate the presence of key named entities from the entity chains in the document and their factual roles — absence usually lowers relevance.

Generalizable approach:
- Map the claim’s fact verification needs onto the entity chains.
- Parse the document to detect mentions of key entities and attributes from the chains.
- Extract and match factual claims (dates, names, relationships, numeric facts) to the claim’s requirements.
- Weigh the document’s factual coverage and correctness about these to assign a relevance score.
- Avoid over-crediting documents lacking explicit or strong connections.

Your final output is only the relevance score for each document paired with concise reasoning explaining that score according to the above principles.

Iteration 29: Proposed new text for program.entity_gap_analyzer.predict: Task: Given a factual claim (usually consisting of multiple interrelated assertions), analyze it to extract multiple distinct entity chains and identify critical information gaps required to verify the claim. Then, generate 2-3 specific search queries that target these entity chains and/or the identified verification gaps. 

Definitions and guidance:

1. Entity Chains  
Entity chains are sequences of connected entities referenced within the claim that form a coherent fact or relation pathway. For example:  
- Person -> Role/Title -> Organization -> Location  
- Film -> Director -> Release Date -> Production Countries  
- Award nominee -> Award event -> Nomination year -> Work nominated  
Extract at least two distinct entity chains per claim that correspond to meaningful factual connections underpinning parts of the claim’s assertions.

2. Information Gaps  
Identify precisely what pieces of information are missing or ambiguous that prevent direct verification of the claim. Typical gaps include:  
- Missing entity identifiers (e.g., unnamed village, unspecified film title, unidentified actress)  
- Lack of clear relationship or timeline data (e.g., dates, roles, production origins)  
- Potential internal inconsistency or ambiguity (e.g., character names confused with real persons, vague references like “the ceremony”, or conflicting identity claims)  
Focus on factual elements that must be confirmed or resolved to support/reject the claim.

3. Query Generation  
Construct 2-3 distinct, targeted, and concise search queries that address separate entity chains or critical information gaps. The queries should:  
- Contain key entity names, roles, documentary keywords, dates, or descriptors from the entity chains  
- Include clarifying terms to disambiguate (e.g., date ranges, event names, alternative spellings)  
- Optionally add example entities or assertions from the claim as anchor points (e.g., a known film title if available)  
- Avoid overly broad or vague queries, but cover different verification angles present in the reasoning  

4. Reasoning and Linking Across Claims  
- Claims often combine multiple interrelated facts; analyze each assertion separately and then consider their links.  
- When claims contain ambiguous or inconsistent references, highlight these and propose queries that can resolve them.  
- Consider domain-specific knowledge as part of reasoning (e.g., recognizing common film titles, award events, organizational affiliations, historical figures, or taxonomy of dog breeds).  
- Use knowledge of naming conventions, production companies, award ceremonies, and similar domain data to formulate precise entity chains and information gaps.  

5. Output Format  
For each claim:  
- Provide a detailed reasoning paragraph outlining the analysis of the claim’s key facts and identification of missing or ambiguous information.  
- List 2-3 distinct entity chains representing key factual threads extracted from the claim, explicitly noting the relations/entities in order.  
- Generate 2-3 distinct search queries tailored to verify these chains or resolve gaps.

6. Notes on Domain-Specific Details  
- For biographical claims, verify personal names, stage names, roles, and chronology.  
- For film-related claims, extract director, actor, film titles, production years, and filming locations.  
- For award claims, identify award names, ceremony editions/years, nominees, and categories.  
- For historical or art claims, resolve ambiguities including familial relations and monument identifications.  
- For music-related claims, verify composers, song titles, genres, and relevant commercial uses.  
- For legal or social claims, verify events, dates, and contrasting entities.  
- For ambiguous or possibly incorrect claims, highlight inconsistencies and propose searches to clarify or refute.  

This thorough approach ensures verifiable, well-structured supporting data queries that cover each critical angle needed to fact-check or support the original claim.
