[AGENT] Change request (full): **Implement Gap-Aware Query Decomposition Architecture with Iterative Gap Analysis**

Replace the current sequential 3-hop retrieval architecture in `langProBe/hover/hover_pipeline.py` with a **gap-aware query decomposition system** that analyzes what information is missing before generating each query, inspired by ITER-RETGEN and recent query decomposition research.

**Key Changes to HoverMultiHopPipeline:**

1. **Create new DSPy Signature: `ClaimDecomposer`**
   - Input: claim (str)
   - Output: sub_queries (list[str]) with max 3 sub-queries
   - Purpose: Decompose complex claims into 2-3 independent sub-queries targeting different entities/facts

2. **Create new DSPy Signature: `GapAnalyzer`**
   - Input: claim (str), retrieved_docs (list[str]), current_coverage (str)
   - Output: missing_info (str), next_query (str)
   - Purpose: Identify what information is still missing and generate targeted query to fill gap

3. **Restructure `forward()` method** to use parallel decomposition + gap-aware sequential retrieval:
   - Phase 1: Decompose claim into 2-3 sub-queries using `dspy.ChainOfThought(ClaimDecomposer)`
   - Phase 2: Retrieve k=25 docs per sub-query in parallel (max 75 docs total across 3 queries, respecting the 3-query constraint)
   - Phase 3: Analyze coverage gaps using `dspy.ChainOfThought(GapAnalyzer)` - identify which entities/facts from supporting_facts are missing
   - Phase 4: If gaps exist and queries remain, generate 1 targeted gap-filling query to retrieve missing docs
   - Phase 5: Apply sliding-window reranking to all retrieved documents (75-100 total) to select final 21

4. **Remove HoverMultiHop dependency** - implement all logic directly in HoverMultiHopPipeline's forward() method to avoid unnecessary module nesting

**Why this works:** Current architecture loses information through sequential summarization and query drift. The new architecture: (a) decomposes claims to target ALL entities upfront, (b) retrieves in parallel to avoid error cascades, (c) explicitly identifies missing information through gap analysis, (d) uses final query budget strategically to fill documented gaps rather than blindly continuing multi-hop chain. This addresses the primary failure patterns: multi-entity handling, information loss, and 4-doc claims.

**Academic basis:** Query decomposition (ACL 2025), ITER-RETGEN gap analysis (RAG survey 2025), and structured query expansion (DeCoR, ACM 2025).

Iteration 31: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: Given three input text fields: `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`, your task is to generate a `query` field that guides information retrieval or verification steps required to establish the factual correctness or verify the claim. 

The `claim` is a factual or comparative statement whose veracity you must evaluate. The two provided summaries, `summary_1` and `summary_2`, are independent assessments or evidence syntheses about the claim based on available knowledge or passages, indicating whether the claim is supported, partially supported, unsupported, or has insufficient evidence.

Your goal is to analyze these inputs together and produce a focused, effective search or research `query` that would:

- Retrieve authoritative and specific information sources to verify all key components of the claim.
- Explicitly target the primary factual elements, entities, dates, relationships, or comparisons mentioned in the claim.
- If the summaries conflict or are incomplete, the query should aim to resolve those conflicts by encompassing all missing or contradictory information.
- For partially supported claims, the query should cover the unresolved facets or claim parts needing additional evidence.
- Use keywords, named entities, dates, relevant titles, and associated terms (e.g., “birth date”, “filmography”, “awards”, “ownership stake”, “discography”, “nationality”, etc.) to ensure precision.
- When multiple elements appear in the claim, structure the query to request information for each one or their interrelation clearly.
- Avoid overly broad queries; instead, be targeted and granular enough to yield the exact facts required.
- In comparative claims, direct the query to collect explicit quantitative or verifiable data on both sides for valid comparison.
- Clearly indicate the type of source expected (e.g., official biographies, trusted databases such as IMDb, discographies, encyclopedias, news archives, film credits) when relevant for verification.

Domain-specific factual aspects to consider include (but are not limited to):

- Precise birth dates and places of people, especially in comparative birth-order claims.
- Filmography and award histories for filmmakers and musicians.
- Official album counts, tracklists, and artist discographies.
- Corporate ownership and shareholding details in airline and franchise arrangements.
- Accurate identification of individuals, group memberships, and roles (e.g., voice type in music groups).
- Verifying historical geopolitical names and their relevance to individual's biographical data.
- Correct identification and differentiation of similarly named entities (e.g., distinguishing "African Cats" from "American Cats").
- Detailed linkage of named entities to works (films, albums, books) and related persons.
- Correct attribution of honors and inclusion in critical or fan lists (e.g., top vocalists).
- Verification of filming locations and associations with specific films and crew members.
- Precise editorial focus and audience demographics for publications.
- Taxonomic classifications and alphabetical ordering in scientific contexts.

Always consider the nuances from the summaries that indicate missing evidence, contradictions between sources, or factual inaccuracies. The query should help retrieve primary or reliable secondary sources that can resolve these issues conclusively.

In sum, generate a clear, precise, multi-part search prompt tailored to gather the minimal yet sufficient facts to verify every claim facet, referencing entities and concepts explicitly mentioned or implied in the inputs.

Iteration 32: Proposed new text for program.confidence_evaluator.predict: You will be given a claim, a set of entity chains relevant to the claim, and multiple retrieved documents. Your task is to assess whether the retrieved documents provide sufficient evidence to verify the claim.

To do this, systematically analyze coverage of the entity chains, facts, and relationships mentioned in the claim within the retrieved documents. Consider these aspects carefully:

1. **Entity Chain and Fact Coverage**  
   - Identify which entity chains or facts are fully or well-covered in the retrieved documents (e.g., a named individual’s biography, a film’s director and cast, a sports club’s league membership).  
   - Detect missing or insufficient coverage of key entities, bridging entities, relationships, or facts critical to verifying the claim.  
   - Take into account indirect references, synonyms, variations in names, ambiguous or incomplete entity chain coverage.  

2. **Disambiguation and Ambiguity Detection**  
   - Resolve or highlight ambiguity in claims or entity references (e.g., multiple people named "Dave Evans"; different adaptations of a title; confusion between similarly named organizations).  
   - When multiple interpretations of the claim or entity chains exist, clearly state which interpretation is supported or unsupported by evidence.  

3. **Fact Types to Consider**  
   - Personal biographical details (birthdate, nationality, roles)  
   - Professional credits (film roles, direction, production, authorship)  
   - Organizational affiliations (sports clubs, schools, leagues, neighborhood governance)  
   - Temporal qualifiers (e.g., “currently”) and their verification via date-specific evidence  
   - Sponsorship relations vs. club/team status (e.g., distinguishing if a football club is a sponsor or a competitor)  

4. **Evidence Quality and Sufficiency**  
   - Distinguish between partial, insufficient, ambiguous, and direct, authoritative evidence.  
   - Recognize when critical connections (such as a sponsorship) are never explicitly stated even if related entities appear.  
   - Consider the depth required to confidently verify a nuanced or complex claim (e.g., comparing who plays more instruments, detailed film cast billing).  

5. **Generate Output**  
   - Provide a clear reasoning summary that:  
     - Lists what is supported explicitly by the documents.  
     - Lists what is missing, ambiguous, or unsupported, including unresolved disambiguations.  
     - Notes any contradictions within the retrieved documents related to the claim.  
   - Assign a confidence score from 0 to 100 indicating how sufficient the evidence is to verify the claim.   
     - Scores closer to 100 indicate strong, direct, and unambiguous support.  
     - Scores closer to 0 indicate absence of evidence or contradicting information.  
   - Provide detailed missing information points needed to improve verification or resolve ambiguities, such as:  
     - Specific missing entity-fact connections or relationships.  
     - Required authoritative sources or types of documents (e.g., official cast lists, league sponsorship pages, discography liner notes).  
     - Clarification points for ambiguous names, titles, dates, or roles.  
     - Suggestions for next retrievals to fill gaps.  

In addition, adhere to the following domain-specific considerations observed in past examples:  
- Film/TV claims often require verifying director, cast (especially billing order for “starred”), production credits, and date/year alignment.  
- Music claims require confirming album release years, credited roles (e.g., instruments played, production), and resolving ambiguous artist names.  
- Sports claims require distinguishing between club participant status versus sponsor status, verifying current participation with season/year qualifiers, and local league governance or sponsorship structures.  
- Geographic claims often need precise location disambiguation, differentiating place names (e.g., railway station locality versus namesake town), and accurate distance measurements referencing official road or community boundaries.  
- Institutional claims require understanding differences between similarly named entities (e.g., academic research institutes vs. media productions), and confirming or rejecting affiliations.  
- Be mindful of nuances around sponsorship naming conventions, e.g., “Ford Motors” (a club) vs. “Ford Motor Company” (a corporation).  

Overall, your goal is to provide an expert-level evidence assessment for complex factual claims by thoroughly analyzing and summarizing coverage in retrieved documents, identifying gaps, ambiguities, contradictions, and providing precise confidence scoring with actionable missing information guidance.

Iteration 34: Proposed new text for reranker.predict: Task: Given a claim and a set of documents/passages, evaluate the relevance of each document to the claim with detailed chain-of-thought reasoning, and produce a ranked list of the documents in descending order of relevance.

Detailed requirements:

1. Input:
   - A claim: an assertion or statement that requires verification or contextual understanding.
   - A collection of documents or passages, each indexed (0-based).

2. Objective:
   - Analyze each document’s relevance to the claim by considering:
     a. How directly the document addresses or relates to the claim’s key elements (e.g., named entities, relationships, events).
     b. Whether the document provides supporting evidence for or contradicts the claim.
     c. The quality, specificity, and uniqueness of the information in the document relevant to the claim.
     d. The presence of critical facts such as dates, names, locations, titles, and their relationships that are important to verify the claim.

3. Approach:
   - Identify and extract key entities and facts from the claim.
   - Search for documents that reference these entities or related concepts explicitly or implicitly.
   - Evaluate for each document:
       i. Degree of direct connection to the claim’s core assertion.
       ii. Presence of specific details (e.g., birthdates, event timelines, roles, titles).
       iii. Whether the passage confirms or denies parts of the claim, or provides context necessary for interpretation.
   - Use chain-of-thought reasoning to justify the relevance ordering, explicitly stating your logic about why a document is ranked higher or lower.

4. Output:
   - A ranked list (array) of document indices (0-based), ordered from most relevant to least relevant with respect to verifying or understanding the claim.

5. Notes and domain specifics gathered from examples:
   - "Supporting facts" often include documents containing multiple supporting entities: named individuals, events, or relations mentioned in the claim.
   - Documents vary in focus: some are biographical (birthdates, careers), some are about works (films, plays, operas), some about awards or relationships (roles, collaborations).
   - Accurate disambiguation is essential: some passages may mention similarly named entities, or related persons/events; discerning which are relevant requires assessing explicit connections.
   - Temporal information (dates, timelines), geographical context, and relationship mappings (e.g., who directed whom, who played which role) are crucial for assessing relevance.
   - Relevance is graded by the document’s contribution to verifying the claim, not just lexical overlap.
   - The task requires understanding multi-faceted claims where more than one entity or fact might need to be verified or compared.
   - Chain-of-thought reasoning must indicate both positive support and contradictions found in documents, to justify inclusion or ranking.

6. Strategy hints:
   - Map claim entities to documents referencing those entities.
   - Prioritize documents that contain direct statements about claim elements.
   - Include documents that provide key contextual or background information that is central to disambiguation.
   - Theory of relevance should incorporate the specificity of information (e.g., exact birth year vs vague mention).
   - Challenge documents or contradictory evidence should be recognized but possibly ranked lower unless they critically refute the claim.

This structured instruction encapsulates the complexity and domain knowledge required for the task and the thoughtful reasoning process demonstrated by the assistant in examples.
